<div id="review_34099276" class="review" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/34099276" />
    <a title="Nicholas Karpuk" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/1574263-nicholas-karpuk"><img alt="Nicholas Karpuk" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1442509877p2/1574263.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/34099276?book_show_action=true">Sep 28, 2008</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Nicholas Karpuk" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Nicholas Karpuk" href="/user/show/1574263-nicholas-karpuk">Nicholas Karpuk</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="really liked it"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">really liked it</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span></span>


          <div><span class="uitext greyText">Recommends it for: </span>
           <span class="uitext reviewText">Hooligans!</span></div>

          <div>
    <span class="greyText">Recommended to Nicholas by: </span>
      Wikipedia
  </div>


    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer34099276" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer11461075458842718800">I wasn't aware of this previously, but apparently you just gotta slap a dame when they get hysterical. The things you learn when you read hard-boiled fiction.<br/><br/>"The Thin Man" was read as an attempt to get into the mindset of noir, since a friend of mine is asking me to write him a script in the style. It's one of my first encounters with crime fiction from that era, and I came away generally amused.<br/><br/>Nick Charles is on vacation with his wife Nora. He doesn't want to solve a mystery. He wants to drin</span>
  <span id="freeText11461075458842718800" style="display:none">I wasn't aware of this previously, but apparently you just gotta slap a dame when they get hysterical. The things you learn when you read hard-boiled fiction.<br /><br />"The Thin Man" was read as an attempt to get into the mindset of noir, since a friend of mine is asking me to write him a script in the style. It's one of my first encounters with crime fiction from that era, and I came away generally amused.<br /><br />Nick Charles is on vacation with his wife Nora. He doesn't want to solve a mystery. He wants to drink, and have fun, and quite specifically not get answers. The trouble is that people he's acquainted with keep showing up, because apparently he's the right man for the job when a woman shows up dead.<br /><br />Dashiel Hammet is a good man to read if you want to study fun dialogue. Nick and Nora engage in the sort of banter that we normal people wish we were having. His stalwart refusal to tell anyone exactly what he may or may not know leads to lots of enjoyable circuitous dialogue in which people keep pressing him for information.<br /><br />There are some real hallmarks of the era in this book. For starters, the aforementioned sexism, which fortunately is counter-balanced by Nora, who's actually a surprisingly progressive character. She tags along through most of the investigation providing sass and observations, and it made me wish more detectives had such classy accompaniment.<br /><br />What really took me by surprise was the odd sophistication of it. Most of the story is about conversations, and it really is a study of manners and class as Charles goes between well-off families in nice rooms to thugs and goons in run down apartments and speakeasies. It's a well rounded perspective on the city life of the area.<br /><br />The drinking is also amusing. By any normal standard most of the characters would be drunk through most of the story. I think it may have been fantasy at the time, since the book was released not long before prohibition was ended, and people wanted to read about rich people getting plastered in nice hotel rooms.<br /><br />Here's a game you can play at home while reading: Take a shot every time a character in the book takes a drink. See how long it takes you before you experience alcohol poisoning. <br /></span>
  <a data-text-id="11461075458842718800" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

<div id="review_2462447509" class="review nosyndicate" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/2462447509" />
    <a title="T.D. Whittle" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/6051061-t-d-whittle"><img alt="T.D. Whittle" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1532773499p2/6051061.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/2462447509?book_show_action=true">Jul 19, 2018</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="T.D. Whittle" class="user" itemprop="url" name="T.D. Whittle" href="/user/show/6051061-t-d-whittle">T.D. Whittle</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="really liked it"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">really liked it</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span></span>

          <span class="greyText">&nbsp;&middot;&nbsp;</span>
          <a class="lightGreyText" title="Paperback " href="/book/show/21468551-the-thin-man">review of another edition</a>


        

          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/6051061-t-d-whittle?shelf=reviews">reviews</a>
          </div>
    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer2462447509" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer4549643434293661783">I had never read a Hammett novel until now and, while I did enjoy it, I would be lying by omission if I did not say I like the movies so much better. I suspect this would be the case, too, with Hammett's other famous novels, in which his snappy detectives are the most enjoyable and memorable aspect of the stories. Hammett's writing is as crisp and clean as fresh-pressed linen, which suits his material. I found the book to be a real page-turner, in many ways.<br/><br/><i>And yet . . . </i> *<br/><br/><img src='https://i.gr-assets.com/images/S/compressed.photo.goodreads.com/hostedimages/1536745277i/26290541.jpg' width='400' height='303' alt='William Powell and Myrna Loy as Nick and Nora Charles, Skippy as Asta' class='gr-hostedUserImg'/> <br/><br/>I think whether reade</span>
  <span id="freeText4549643434293661783" style="display:none">I had never read a Hammett novel until now and, while I did enjoy it, I would be lying by omission if I did not say I like the movies so much better. I suspect this would be the case, too, with Hammett's other famous novels, in which his snappy detectives are the most enjoyable and memorable aspect of the stories. Hammett's writing is as crisp and clean as fresh-pressed linen, which suits his material. I found the book to be a real page-turner, in many ways.<br><br><i>And yet . . . </i> *<br><br><img src="https://i.gr-assets.com/images/S/compressed.photo.goodreads.com/hostedimages/1536745277i/26290541.jpg" width="400" height="303" alt="William Powell and Myrna Loy as Nick and Nora Charles, Skippy as Asta" class="gr-hostedUserImg"> <br><br>I think whether readers enjoy Hammet's writing must depend on whether they like their hard-boiled detective stories in the form of novels because it's a specific kind of fiction, stylistically. Hammett was apparently the hard-boiled writer par excellence, the one to whom all others were compared. I haven't read many of these types of books before. I read people like Agatha Christie and Dorothy Sayers when I'm in the mood for vintage mysteries. Hammett's writing is high quality but aesthetically minimalist, almost terse at times. The book is nearly all action and dialogue with little to no reflection. I might have liked this more in my youth when I was a big fan of Hemingway and his highly-restrained style. (It's very obvious in reading Hammett that Hemingway was a fan, by the way.) These days, I prefer less focus on action and more quiet reflection and internal dialogue, at least in the books I read. I am different about movies.<br><br><img src="https://i.gr-assets.com/images/S/compressed.photo.goodreads.com/hostedimages/1536745277i/26290540._SY540_.jpg" width="400" height="497" alt="William Powell and Myrna Loy as Nick and Nora Charles, Skippy as Asta" class="gr-hostedUserImg"><br><br>When it comes to films, I am happy with lots of action and snappy dialogue. Some of my favourite movies are <i>Auntie Mame</i> (not the musical but the 1958 one starring Rosalind Russell), <i>Philadelphia Story</i>, <i>My Man Godfrey</i>, and <i>Harvey</i> which are all fast-paced and brimming with witty banter. Besides loving vintage screwball comedies, I just spent a long flight from L.A. to Melbourne binge-watching all the Bogart-and-Bacall movies I could find on the entertainment module, though of course I've already seen them all more than once. They never disappoint but I think it's not that the stories themselves are so gripping. It's the smoke coming off the screen whenever the two of them are together that rivets the attention. And really, can anyone say they prefer reading <a href="https://www.goodreads.com/book/show/29999.The_Maltese_Falcon" title="The Maltese Falcon by Dashiell Hammett" rel="nofollow noopener">The Maltese Falcon</a> to watching Bogart play the role of Sam Spade?<br><br>William Powell (Nick), Myrna Loy (Nora), and Asta (Skippy) were perfectly cast. Powell and Loy had a playful, sexy chemistry between them that bounces off the screen even now, so many decades on. Both actors have wonderfully expressive faces, flawless delivery, and perfect timing―which is the only kind of timing you can have in comedy of course.<br><br><img src="https://i.gr-assets.com/images/S/compressed.photo.goodreads.com/hostedimages/1536745277i/26290543.png" width="400" height="297" alt="Myrna Loy and William Powell as Nick and Nora Charles" class="gr-hostedUserImg"><br><br>I would have to agree with <a href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/2169342210?book_show_action=false&amp;from_review_page=1" rel="nofollow noopener">Paul Bryant's review</a>, too, that most of the witty repartee is in the film, not the book. The surprising (for the time) sensuality is intact in the book, rather like a water gun that sprays every attractive person within a ten-foot radius. Everybody notices everybody else's sex appeal; everyone drinks constantly; no one ever expresses themselves openly and honestly except Asta, the Schnauzer, who cannot help herself. (She morphs into a male wire-haired terrier in the film but just as endearing, because dogs just are, aren't they?)<br><br>I have to go back and re-watch the movies now. I have not seen them in years. I had forgot who actually perpetrated the series of crimes, so that did come as a surprise. I could not work it out, since no one ever talks straight in this book. Nick, despite drinking heavily and having to wrestle down women and punch men from time to time, managed to work out all the details in the end. I didn't care for the very last line of the book, but perhaps I am too particular about such things. Last lines, to me, are as important as first ones. The final line of the book is Nora saying, "That may be ... but it's all pretty unsatisfactory," which left me with the feeling that she hadn't quite liked the way Nick had come to his conclusions in this very messy case. As final lines go, I found her response pretty unsatisfactory!<br><br><img src="https://i.gr-assets.com/images/S/compressed.photo.goodreads.com/hostedimages/1536745277i/26290542.jpg" width="400" height="495" alt="Myrna Loy and William Powell as Nick and Nora Charles" class="gr-hostedUserImg"><br><br>* Nicole Krauss, <a href="https://www.goodreads.com/book/show/3867.The_History_of_Love" title="The History of Love by Nicole Krauss" rel="nofollow noopener">The History of Love</a></span>
  <a data-text-id="4549643434293661783" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>

    <div class="section">
      

<div id="review_233767308" class="review" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/233767308" />
    <a title="Kirk" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/667059-kirk"><img alt="Kirk" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1442453577p2/667059.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/233767308?book_show_action=true">Nov 10, 2011</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Kirk" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Kirk" href="/user/show/667059-kirk">Kirk</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="liked it"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">liked it</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span></span>

          <span class="greyText">&nbsp;&middot;&nbsp;</span>
          <a class="lightGreyText" title="ebook 9780307767509" href="/book/show/10102781-the-thin-man">review of another edition</a>


        

    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer233767308" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer3655586325081418042">If I weren't the mean teacher I am I'd cut Hammett slack and round up to four stars because this---the last of his novels---is a solid 3-1/2. And that's only because it pales in comparison to every one of his other novels except THE DAIN CURSE, which is the true 3-star. <br/><br/>The main knock on THIN is that Hammett was pretty much bored with fiction by this point in his career, having lost his bearings to booze, broads, and just about any other indulgence that began with a B except Billy Barty (too sho</span>
  <span id="freeText3655586325081418042" style="display:none">If I weren't the mean teacher I am I'd cut Hammett slack and round up to four stars because this---the last of his novels---is a solid 3-1/2. And that's only because it pales in comparison to every one of his other novels except THE DAIN CURSE, which is the true 3-star. <br><br>The main knock on THIN is that Hammett was pretty much bored with fiction by this point in his career, having lost his bearings to booze, broads, and just about any other indulgence that began with a B except Billy Barty (too short for him). And those lost bearings are evident pretty much on every page. For starters, the plot isn't all that thrillsville, especially not compared to RED HARVEST or THE GLASS KEY. For a guy who made it a point to do away with the Sherlock Holmes/drawing room villain-unveiling that was a mainstay of the British Golden Era (as well as S.S. Van Dine, etc), Nick and Nora's final dinner party seems snipped straight from Agatha Christie. Also the book isn't anywhere near as hardboiled as the other novels, probably because Hammett was writing about a happily married man with a rich wife and therefore nothing to be disillusioned about. But that lack of beefstake points to a major flaw: Hammett doesn't really know what to do with Nora in here, except to have her take the dog Asta for a walk. She's pretty much a cipher, nowhere as near as lively or vivacious as Myrna Loy.<br><br>The real sogginess here, though, is the booze. As many critics have noted, you'd be hard-pressed to find a "classic" novel where the drinks flow this constantly. Seriously, by page 20 my blood alcohol was edging toward Rick Perry territory. My wife took my keys away from me by Chapter 8. By the denouement I was so blotto I not only let Snooki take me home but found her sorta cute, too. This isn't a Lost Territory sort of drunkenness, either. No Jake Barnes lamenting the loss of moral rigor while surrounded by rummies, no Geoffrey Firmin going off all Vesuvius-like in great bursts of dipsomania grandiloquence. This is pure being-drunky-skunky-is-fun drunkenness. Dean Martin might have commended the verisimilitude, but for readers who can't pop a second beer without warnings about alcoholism flashing in the distillery of their minds, it's probably presented a little too glibly to take comfortably.<br><br>That said, Hammett hits a home run in at least one influential way. With Nick and Nora he created the prototype of the wisecracking husband-and-wife detective team, one that will be ripped off endlessly in years to come in Hart to Hart, etc etc. The charm of the book is how un-threatened the Charles's marriage is by the venality of modern life. Hell, Nick can even wrestle a femme fatale to the ground and his wife's first response will be to ask if the tussle gave him a stiffie. (His answer: "Oh, a little"---come on, man! have confidence!). Even if Nora is only around to solve the mystery of her husband's tumescence, the repartee is the book's genius, and the tale is best enjoyed for it rather than any thrill or hardboiled sting.<br><br>The original cover of the book featured a picture of Hammett in his usual natty attire:<br><span>[image error]</span> <br>It's a pretty good indicative of his fame at the time but also how trapped he was in the image he'd created for his writing. Most folks who read the book for the first time are surprised Nick isn't the titular character in all his svelte fashion stylin'. Instead, the thin man is a rather soupy victim we never really care about. And the book lacks for a major villain as interesting as Joel Cairo or that fat bastard Gutman (!) in THE MALTESE FALCON. The movie follows the plot and actually to my thinking works better. Still, THIN is an entertaining diversion if you can keep sober til the end. Hammett certainly couldn't.</span>
  <a data-text-id="3655586325081418042" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

<div id="review_6034813" class="review" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/6034813" />
    <a title="Sarah" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/48216-sarah"><img alt="Sarah" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1392259462p2/48216.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/6034813?book_show_action=true">Sep 11, 2007</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Sarah" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Sarah" href="/user/show/48216-sarah">Sarah</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="it was amazing"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">it was amazing</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span></span>


          <div><span class="uitext greyText">Recommends it for: </span>
           <span class="uitext reviewText">detectives, alcoholics</span></div>

        

    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer6034813" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer17056957731561065468">Somehow I never saw this movie or read this book during my six-month crime noir kick in ninth grade (though I did read Hammett's The Maltese Falcon and The Glass Key aroud that time). But, boy, I'm glad that I've read it now. <br/><br/>The Thin Man is the last novel Hammett completed (though he started or pretended to start a half-dozen others) and it has the feel of being a parady of his other novellas and the 1930s crime genre in general. It is fabulously funny - as in, I couldn't go two pages without t</span>
  <span id="freeText17056957731561065468" style="display:none">Somehow I never saw this movie or read this book during my six-month crime noir kick in ninth grade (though I did read Hammett's The Maltese Falcon and The Glass Key aroud that time). But, boy, I'm glad that I've read it now. <br /><br />The Thin Man is the last novel Hammett completed (though he started or pretended to start a half-dozen others) and it has the feel of being a parady of his other novellas and the 1930s crime genre in general. It is fabulously funny - as in, I couldn't go two pages without trying to read something outloud to Ben. As in the protagonists, Nick and Nora Charles, have the most wonderful, fun, and loving time ever while they solve murders together. I'm talking about more drinking, more intrigue, more mysertious dames, and more witty dialogue than all of his other works put together. <br /><br />Of course, these were my thoughts before reading the detailed chronology/short biography of Hammett in the back of the book. Surely, I thought while reading the novel, much of the humor comes from the ABSURD number of martinis, affairs, suspects, and drama that clutters every page. But after reading about Dashiell Hammett's life, it seems like this might have been his most realistic novel. <br /><br />His chronology includes, in between novel releases and movie adaptations, such phrases as "1931: drinks heavily and has many affairs." "1920: serves as Pinkerton Operative." "1947: moves in with daughter Mary Jane; both drink heavily." "1953: Questioned by Senator McCarthy about donating royalties to the Communist Party." "1932: Found guilty of battery and rape of actress Elise De Vianne." "1934: Signs contract with MGM in october for a second movie in the Thin Man series. Drinks heavily." <br /><br />This chronology goes on for TEN PAGES and includes more affairs, benders, posh apartments, debt collectors, trials, political intrigue, and detective work than in an entire season of Law &amp; Order. <br /><br />It made me see the book a bit differently - to appreciate it even more, really. For Hammett to treat these subjects lightly - subjects that were at the very time tearing his life and career apart - give the book the feeling of a fantasy - a sort of fairy tale noir. As if Nick and Nora Charles live in a parallel universe where "cutting the phlegm" with a few whiskys in the morning is okay, where smooth talking can get you out of trouble with creditors and the law, and where bullets always only manage to graze and annoy you. <br /><br />I like to imagine Hammett - with all of the drama of his life swirling around him - smiling and laughing as he wrote his novel. Just really having a good time. I don't know if that's true or not, but I had a good time reading it. </span>
  <a data-text-id="17056957731561065468" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/2125257143?book_show_action=true">Sep 17, 2017</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Christine PNW" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Christine PNW" href="/user/show/5664767-christine-pnw">Christine PNW</a>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="it was ok"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">it was ok</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span></span>

        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer2125257143" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer5178745919884516794">So, this one was a bit underwhelming, to be perfectly honest. I wanted to like it, with it's aura of louche 1930's glamour. Unfortunately, I basically disliked all of the characters except for Nick & Nora. On second thought, I disliked Nick, too.<br/> <br/>I was especially uncomfortable with Dorry, who was depicted as a hormonally-driven drunken teenager, and Nick's reaction to her tight little body made me more than a little uncomfortable (actually, I'm not sure if she was a teenager). Her brother, Gilbe</span>
  <span id="freeText5178745919884516794" style="display:none">So, this one was a bit underwhelming, to be perfectly honest. I wanted to like it, with it's aura of louche 1930's glamour. Unfortunately, I basically disliked all of the characters except for Nick &amp; Nora. On second thought, I disliked Nick, too.<br /> <br />I was especially uncomfortable with Dorry, who was depicted as a hormonally-driven drunken teenager, and Nick's reaction to her tight little body made me more than a little uncomfortable (actually, I'm not sure if she was a teenager). Her brother, Gilbert, was identified as being 18, and I can't remember if Dorothy was older or younger than he was by 2 years - so she was either 16, which is super gross, or she was 20, which is marginally less gross.<br /> <br />With respect to Nick, well, he simply didn't feel real to me. He seemed more like a far cooler version of Dashiell Hammett - a man's man, able to drink tall bottles with a single bound, deflecting bullets with the manliness of his manly chest. Too much wish fulfillment, too little substance.<br /> <br />And Nora. Ah, Nora. Gillian Flynn's Cool Girl, a manic pixie dream dame, Prohibition Edition. She didn't exist except as something for men to ogle over. Even her witty banter was performative. With her red hair and her extreme coolness, I never got a sense of what she would be like in a room by herself. <br /> <br />The mystery was meh.<br /> <br />The dog was cute, though.<br /> <br />I can totally understand how this book could've made a beloved movie, because it is so character driven, and the flaws that I couldn't ignore would've been much less apparent in film. I might make an effort to track it down and compare it to the source material.<br /> <br />So, I'm not sorry I read it, but I didn't enjoy it nearly as much as I enjoyed The Bride Wore Black.</span>
  <a data-text-id="5178745919884516794" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>

<div id="review_2045253756" class="review" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/2045253756" />
    <a title="Bruce Beckham" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/28623020-bruce-beckham"><img alt="Bruce Beckham" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1442874525p2/28623020.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/2045253756?book_show_action=true">Jul 23, 2017</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Bruce Beckham" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Bruce Beckham" href="/user/show/28623020-bruce-beckham">Bruce Beckham</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="it was amazing"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">it was amazing</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span></span>



        

    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer2045253756" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer9038623865674595562">This is a slender book in more ways than its title.<br/><br/>If you are a style aficionado, the thrift with which Dashiell Hammett writes is something to savour. I recall when I read <i>The Maltese Falcon</i> vividly ‘seeing’ each new character he introduced, and <i>The Thin Man</i> is no different. Take this excerpt, for example, describing the uninvited appearance of gangster Shep Morelli in sleuth (& protagonist) Nick Charles’ bedroom doorway:<br/><br/><i>“He was a plump, dark, youngish man of medium height, broad through the ja</i></span>
  <span id="freeText9038623865674595562" style="display:none">This is a slender book in more ways than its title.<br /><br />If you are a style aficionado, the thrift with which Dashiell Hammett writes is something to savour. I recall when I read <i>The Maltese Falcon</i> vividly ‘seeing’ each new character he introduced, and <i>The Thin Man</i> is no different. Take this excerpt, for example, describing the uninvited appearance of gangster Shep Morelli in sleuth (&amp; protagonist) Nick Charles’ bedroom doorway:<br /><br /><i>“He was a plump, dark, youngish man of medium height, broad through the jaws, narrow between the eyes. He wore a black bowler hat, a black overcoat that fitted him very snugly, a dark suit, and black shoes, all looking as if he had bought them within the last fifteen minutes. The gun, a blunt black .38-calibre automatic, lay comfortably in his hand, not pointing at anything.”</i><br /><br />Whew – edit that, if you can!<br /><br />The same rigour applies to his dialogue:<br /><br /><i>Nora said: “I love you, Nicky, because you smell nice and know such fascinating people.”<br />Dorothy said: “It’s not much out of your way to drop me at Mamma’s, is it?”<br />I said: “No,” and gave the driver Mimi’s address.<br />Nora said: “Come home with us.”<br />Dorothy said: “No – o, I’d better not.”<br />Nora asked: “Why not?” and Dorothy said: “Well, I don’t think I ought to,” and that kind of thing went on until the taxicab stopped at the Cortland.</i><br /><br /><i>Nora said... Dorothy said... I said...</i> wooden and repetitive on the face of it, in no time I found myself not noticing, like the reassuring <i>clackety-clack</i> of a train, a hypnotic background rhythm.<br /><br />Copywriting methodology aside, <i>The Thin Man</i> happens to be an ingenious whodunit. Much as the characters continually debate who, what and why – Hammett keeps you guessing – right to the very edge of the cliff – an almost impossibly late moment – before sending you off the precipice into a kind of enlightened free fall that just about gives you time to realise what a dimwit you’ve been (in my case, at least) before you land with a bump.<br /><br />If I had to find fault, it is that the book does not quite end there – when I am sure it could perfectly well. But instead there is a peculiar ‘denouement’ chapter, where sleuth Nick explains to wife Nora exactly what happened – with some anachronistic post-rationalisations from the narrator (Nick himself) thrown in. Personally I would have been content to know the general gist, which is revealed in one brilliant stanza in the penultimate chapter.<br /><br />Nevertheless, a must-read for the student of detective fiction, I should venture.<br /></span>
  <a data-text-id="9038623865674595562" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

<div id="review_7484086" class="review" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/7484086" />
    <a title="Willis Markuske" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/477389-willis-markuske"><img alt="Willis Markuske" src="https://s.gr-assets.com/assets/nophoto/user/m_50x66-82093808bca726cb3249a493fbd3bd0f.png" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/7484086?book_show_action=true">Oct 09, 2007</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Willis Markuske" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Willis Markuske" href="/user/show/477389-willis-markuske">Willis Markuske</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="it was amazing"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">it was amazing</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span></span>



        

    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer7484086" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer12672975057729444506">My favorite Hammett book. Written with the same economical and sparse style of his other novels, the tone couldn't be more different. Nick & Nora Charles are fun characters who come off much more 3 dimensional - as opposed to archetypcal - than either Sam Spade or the Continental Op. <br/><br/>Plot is almost a secondary concern here which is rare for a mystery. Instead the almost constant drinking and flirting the two main characters engage in (with each other and whomever else is around) get top billing </span>
  <span id="freeText12672975057729444506" style="display:none">My favorite Hammett book. Written with the same economical and sparse style of his other novels, the tone couldn't be more different. Nick &amp; Nora Charles are fun characters who come off much more 3 dimensional - as opposed to archetypcal - than either Sam Spade or the Continental Op. <br /><br />Plot is almost a secondary concern here which is rare for a mystery. Instead the almost constant drinking and flirting the two main characters engage in (with each other and whomever else is around) get top billing over the central mystery. <br /><br />Nick is a former detective who wants no part of crime solving but would rather enjoy some heavy drinking in prohibition era NY. But because of his past everyone from the police to the criminals assumes he's on the case until eventually he is. <br /><br />What's really interesting is that even though he's trying to stay out of the whole affair and is downplaying his past to anyone who will listen, you do get a few glimpses and hints that he was probably a very formidable detective. <br /><br />Also interesting is his lack of an obvious "code", something that most other dectives in this era seem to place a large emphasis on. The above mentioned Sam Spade and Continental Op, as well as Phillip Marlowe and Mike Hammer... they all can be counted on to make sure the reader knows they have code of honor. <br /><br />Nick on the other hand is a good detective and good guy who would be more than content to avoid any sleuthing and have several drinks if everyone would just leave him alone. When he finally does decide to take a more active part in the mystery it's with a nicely cynical edge. Not because he is weary or burned out - no this is a fun book. He's just an extremely good detective who knows better than to think it's exciting. <br /><br />One criticism of the book is that Nora (his wife) could probably be fleshed out a bit more. Her main jobs are to alternately prod Nick and tease him while getting him drinks. She also helps to show how cool Nick must be as every man who lays eyes on her is noticably impressed. Add that to the fact that she's over a decade younger than him as well as incredibly rich and she's almost a fantasy figure. <br /><br />At least she doesn't fall into the damsel in distress cliche. And while she isn't a detective by any means, when it comes to wit and personality she's her husbands equal by any measure.<br /><br />Overall it's a great book and very different type of approach to the mystery novel. </span>
  <a data-text-id="12672975057729444506" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>


      <div class="reviewFooter uitext buttons">
          

<tr class="no_border feedFooterReview" id="update_comment_stuff_Review7484086">

<div id="review_185311947" class="review nosyndicate" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/185311947" />
    <a title="Kim" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/4542979-kim"><img alt="Kim" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1454402751p2/4542979.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/185311947?book_show_action=true">Jul 15, 2011</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Kim" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Kim" href="/user/show/4542979-kim">Kim</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="really liked it"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">really liked it</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span></span>

          <span class="greyText">&nbsp;&middot;&nbsp;</span>
          <a class="lightGreyText" title="Kindle Edition " href="/book/show/10303018-the-thin-man">review of another edition</a>


        

          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/4542979-kim?shelf=crime-fiction">crime-fiction</a>
          </div>
    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer185311947" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer16337627136256106312">This is the first Hammett novel I've read. I don't know why I haven't made the effort to read such an iconic writer before, particularly as I am a long-time crime fiction reader and a fan of "classic" mysteries. It may be my first Hammett, but I'm pretty sure it won't be my last.<br/><br/>I've just finished re-reading all of the novels of Dorothy L Sayers, who is without doubt my favourite writer of "Golden Age" mysteries. It was interesting to compare The Thin Man with Sayers' novels. It's certainly less</span>
  <span id="freeText16337627136256106312" style="display:none">This is the first Hammett novel I've read. I don't know why I haven't made the effort to read such an iconic writer before, particularly as I am a long-time crime fiction reader and a fan of "classic" mysteries. It may be my first Hammett, but I'm pretty sure it won't be my last.<br /><br />I've just finished re-reading all of the novels of Dorothy L Sayers, who is without doubt my favourite writer of "Golden Age" mysteries. It was interesting to compare The Thin Man with Sayers' novels. It's certainly less literary and intellectual than much of Sayers' writing, but Hammett and Sayers share an ability to write witty and entertaining dialogue. They are both skilled at summoning up a strong sense of place and time and their plotting is clever. <br /><br />I love the banter between Nick and Nora and the way Hammett conveyed the strength of their relationship without needing to spell it out. I love the novel's elegant style and it's evocation of 1930s New York City. The mystery was strong enough to keep me interested and I didn't guess the culprit in advance of the big reveal. I was initially amazed at all of the references to alcohol consumption and became convinced that the novel would be but a pamphlet if all the references to drinking were removed. But I either stopped noticing it, or else the incidence of drinking decreased as the novel progressed. All in all, this was a very worthwhile read. Now I understand what the fuss about Hammett is actually all about. </span>
  <a data-text-id="16337627136256106312" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

<div id="review_134431546" class="review nosyndicate" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/134431546" />
    <a title=" Danielle The Book Huntress (Back to the Books)" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/1570669-danielle-the-book-huntress-back-to-the-books"><img alt=" Danielle The Book Huntress (Back to the Books)" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1403239075p2/1570669.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/134431546?book_show_action=true">Dec 10, 2010</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title=" Danielle The Book Huntress (Back to the Books)" class="user" itemprop="url" name=" Danielle The Book Huntress (Back to the Books)" href="/user/show/1570669-danielle-the-book-huntress-back-to-the-books"> Danielle The Book Huntress (Back to the Books)</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="really liked it"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">really liked it</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span></span>


          <div><span class="uitext greyText">Recommends it for: </span>
           <span class="uitext reviewText">fans of mystery, classic detective novels</span></div>

        

          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/1570669-danielle-the-book-huntress-back-to-the-books?shelf=classic">classic</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/1570669-danielle-the-book-huntress-back-to-the-books?shelf=library-checkout">library-checkout</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/1570669-danielle-the-book-huntress-back-to-the-books?shelf=2013-200s-club-challenge">2013-200s-club-challenge</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/1570669-danielle-the-book-huntress-back-to-the-books?shelf=fortysomething-lead-character">fortysomething-lead-character</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/1570669-danielle-the-book-huntress-back-to-the-books?shelf=set-in-new-york-city">set-in-new-york-city</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/1570669-danielle-the-book-huntress-back-to-the-books?shelf=epistolary-narrative">epistolary-narrative</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/1570669-danielle-the-book-huntress-back-to-the-books?shelf=married-protagonists">married-protagonists</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/1570669-danielle-the-book-huntress-back-to-the-books?shelf=laughed-myself-silly-while-reading">laughed-myself-silly-while-reading</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/1570669-danielle-the-book-huntress-back-to-the-books?shelf=detective-private-eye-sleuth">detective-private-eye-sleuth</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/1570669-danielle-the-book-huntress-back-to-the-books?shelf=family">family</a>
          </div>
    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer134431546" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer476740015894452484">I will freely admit that part of why I read this book was that I enjoyed what I have seen of the movie so much. I actually didn't get to watch all of it, as I caught it on Turner Classic Movies after it started and wasn't able to watch the whole movie. I made a note that I wanted to read the book and get the whole movie set on DVD at some point. Additionally, I am interested in the roots of the detective novel. You can't explore detective fiction without reading Dashiell Hammett. So here we go..</span>
  <span id="freeText476740015894452484" style="display:none">I will freely admit that part of why I read this book was that I enjoyed what I have seen of the movie so much. I actually didn't get to watch all of it, as I caught it on Turner Classic Movies after it started and wasn't able to watch the whole movie. I made a note that I wanted to read the book and get the whole movie set on DVD at some point. Additionally, I am interested in the roots of the detective novel. You can't explore detective fiction without reading Dashiell Hammett. So here we go....<br /><br />I liked this book. It starts out very well. I was instantly drawn into the story from the first sentence. The writing is crisp and ripe with that heady atmosphere of the early 20th Century (1930s). There is a cynicism apparent in the characterization and the dialogue that speaks of its noir tone. That makes sense in light of the fact that it takes place during The Great Depression and right at the end of the Prohibition years. I was quite surprised at the frank elements of sex, drug/alcohol abuse, and crime, and a hint of police corruption (clearly I haven't read much pulp/noir classic fiction). Sadly, the 'n' word was used, which I could have done without. I have to say I appreciated the unsympathetic portrayal of human nature more than the actual mystery. Hammett's lens of humanity (via Nick) is not at all rose colored, but it's very astute and the characters were well-drawn. Overall, this was very good. While it did have some good twists and turns, it was rather anticlimactic in the end.<br /><br />Nick Charles is a suitably amiable narrator. He seems experienced and wise to the ways of the world, nobody's fool. Yet he isn't completely jaded or lacking in integrity and honor. People seems to like him and open up to him, but he's not a man to take advantage of. While Nick is now retired from private investigation, his acquaintances draw him into a case unwillingly. I think Nick's nose for a mystery leads him the rest of the way. Nick proves that his investigative skills have not weakened in his retirement. I must say that I enjoyed the fact that Nora's a very perceptive woman with a good brain for investigation as well, even though she serves in the capacity of a part-time sidekick to Nick. Available to give a helping hand and a word to point him in the right direction. Hammett teaches me how to write a novel in which the mystery is tag-teamed by two instead of where the main character works alone and always knows more than anyone else.<br /><br />This novel had me laughing a lot initially. Hammett's writing was quite witty, albeit cynical. Nick and Nora definitely like their booze, and have strong opinions on good quality alcohol. Their constant drinking was a source of humor to me, although I did wonder what effects it had on their liver. <br /><br />Not one of the characters in this book is what I would consider well-adjusted, outside of Nora, and possibly Nick. Nora as seen through Nick's eyes doesn't reveal a whole lot about her except that she is very observant and has a nurturing nature (shown in the way she cared for and fussed over Dorothy). She also seems to lack patience for gossipy types, considering her dislike of Tip, a hostess in their social circle. She clearly loves Nick and feels comfortable with him to say what she thinks. She doesn't coddle him, although she does see to his comfort and is affectionate. She keeps things real with him and tells him the truth when he needs to hear it. I enjoyed their banter. Nora seems like a woman of her times, but is neither overly submissive or dominant in a way that would be unlikely for her times. In comparison to other women in the book, she comes off as the ideal mate to a seasoned man of the world--attractive, accepting, intelligent, socially graced, and fun-loving. I found it amusing how captivated the police detective, Guild was with Nora. <br /><br />Mimi, the ex-wife of the missing man that starts the case that this novel revolves around, is a negative contrast. She is calculating and emotionally unstable. Her cruelty towards her daughter and her tendency to manipulate others cancels out her clearly considerable beauty and physical charms. Nick's narrative suggests that she is envious of her daughter (who is described as gorgeous and beautiful for her young years by few of the male characters). Despite these negative traits, she's not quite the quintessential femme fatale one expects to encounter in noir fiction. Dorothy herself was hard to read. She seems to lack emotional stability, but that makes sense in light of the abuse she suffers with her mother, and the fact that she probably gets far too much and unwanted attention from men for her young age. She latches onto Nick and Nora as a substitute parental unit, as they represent stability that she has lacked in her family life. Other characters also have a rather vivid life, despite the shorter length of this novel. As any good mystery writer, Hammett gives the reader a healthy list of suspects from which to choose the culprit, and I didn't guess who it was until Nick reveals the murderer. <br /><br />As I said earlier, I found the denouement rather lacking in tension, which did dim my enjoyment a bit. Additionally, this book falls into periods of expansive dialogue towards the end that felt a bit tedious. Despite those shortcomings, this was still an enjoyable book. I would have to agree that this book is quite different in feel from the movie. I wonder if that is because of the Movie Codes. I don't think they could have gotten away with putting some of the more frank elements in this novel in the movie version, so they played up the witty banter and humorous elements from the novel.<br /><br />All in all, I enjoyed my first exposure to Dashiell Hammett. I felt truly immersed in this time period and I liked Nick and Nora as main detectives. I like reading about main characters who are married, and this is definitely one to recommend to readers who enjoy this theme. I will be reading The Maltese Falcon in the near future.</span>
  <a data-text-id="476740015894452484" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>

<div id="review_31075994" class="review" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/31075994" />
    <a title="Krok Zero" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/1373169-krok-zero"><img alt="Krok Zero" src="https://s.gr-assets.com/assets/nophoto/user/m_50x66-82093808bca726cb3249a493fbd3bd0f.png" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/31075994?book_show_action=true">Aug 24, 2008</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Krok Zero" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Krok Zero" href="/user/show/1373169-krok-zero">Krok Zero</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="it was ok"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">it was ok</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span></span>



        

          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/1373169-krok-zero?shelf=winter-10-to-11">winter-10-to-11</a>
          </div>
    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer31075994" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer11458972864296314826">Man, what the hell? This book kind of sucks for some reason. Everyone knows that Hammett pretty much single-handedly invented modern crime fiction, and <i>The Maltese Falcon</i> is an enduring masterpiece that may still stand as the most geometrically perfect example of the detective novel form. <i>The Thin Man</i> is his second most famous work, owing to the popular Hollywood film series loosely based on characters therein, but it is an undistinguished, amateurish work that does not hold up on its own terms.</span>
  <span id="freeText11458972864296314826" style="display:none">Man, what the hell? This book kind of sucks for some reason. Everyone knows that Hammett pretty much single-handedly invented modern crime fiction, and <i>The Maltese Falcon</i> is an enduring masterpiece that may still stand as the most geometrically perfect example of the detective novel form. <i>The Thin Man</i> is his second most famous work, owing to the popular Hollywood film series loosely based on characters therein, but it is an undistinguished, amateurish work that does not hold up on its own terms. (This gives me no pleasure to report, I should add. I assumed this would be great and I'm really disappointed that it isn't.)<br /><br />The novel has the feel of a pulp quickie that was dashed off for cash, and a bit of research confirms that that's basically what it was in Hammett's eyes. The mystery unfolds as a series of exposition-packed conversations, none of which are written with much sparkle or personality. Nick and Nora Charles became Hollywood icons by virtue of their cosmopolitan charisma, witty repartee and fashionable drunkenness, but those traits are so marginalized in Hammett's novel that credit for the characters' iconic status belongs more to the unremembered screenwriters (and Wiliam Powell and Myrna Loy) than to Hammett himself. In fact, Nora Charles barely appears in the novel at all — it is mostly about Nick getting mixed up with a kooky family and sifting through their lies to solve a related murder. It is sporadically engaging, but just as often not, and I really didn't care about the central mystery or its resolution. Nor is there any of the moral weight that made <i>The Maltese Falcon</i> a classic.<br /><br />This book is a strike against D.H. in the eternal war of Hammett vs. Chandler — the booklover's equivalent of the Chaplin vs. Keaton argument. But I still need to read <i>The Glass Key</i>, which I've heard was Hammett's favorite of his own books, before I make up my mind.</span>
  <a data-text-id="11458972864296314826" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

<div id="review_86577949" class="review" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/86577949" />
    <a title="Mike (the Paladin)" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/2187043-mike-the-paladin"><img alt="Mike (the Paladin)" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1528431476p2/2187043.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/86577949?book_show_action=true">Jan 24, 2010</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Mike (the Paladin)" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Mike (the Paladin)" href="/user/show/2187043-mike-the-paladin">Mike (the Paladin)</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="liked it"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">liked it</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span></span>



        

    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer86577949" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer15899961860211244716">Okay...if you're into "hard-boiled" detective fiction or mystery fiction I'm sure you'd rate this book higher. Maybe for me it would even be a 3.5 if I could go there.<br/><br/>The dialogue held me here, the by play and banter between Nick, Nora, and then the entire cast of characters. It was well written and well characterized. I suppose it was also well plotted only, I just don't seem to be a mystery fan. I had to keep dragging my interest back to the book. Maybe the fact that I loved the movie and ther</span>
  <span id="freeText15899961860211244716" style="display:none">Okay...if you're into "hard-boiled" detective fiction or mystery fiction I'm sure you'd rate this book higher. Maybe for me it would even be a 3.5 if I could go there.<br /><br />The dialogue held me here, the by play and banter between Nick, Nora, and then the entire cast of characters. It was well written and well characterized. I suppose it was also well plotted only, I just don't seem to be a mystery fan. I had to keep dragging my interest back to the book. Maybe the fact that I loved the movie and therefore already know <a class="jsShowSpoiler spoilerAction">(view spoiler)</a><span class="spoilerContainer" style="display: none">[the "Thin Man" of the title was already dead and couldn't be the murderer.  <a class="jsHideSpoiler spoilerAction">(hide spoiler)</a>]</span>I don't know. But judging by my own past and what I know of my own taste...I just don't get into mysteries...even the good ones. And (note how daring I am in starting the last sentence with "But" and this one with "And") anyway...and I'd say this is a good one. I tried to read the Maltese Falcon a while back with the same result. I just couldn't get into it.<br /><br />However I did get through it and found the actual writing quite good. I would bet real mystery fans will love it. So 3 stars because I had to force my interest back to it time after time, but some will definitely like it more than that. </span>
  <a data-text-id="15899961860211244716" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

<div id="review_2355572121" class="review" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/2355572121" />
    <a title="Debbie Robson" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/1743317-debbie-robson"><img alt="Debbie Robson" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1493107767p2/1743317.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/2355572121?book_show_action=true">Jul 19, 2018</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Debbie Robson" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Debbie Robson" href="/user/show/1743317-debbie-robson">Debbie Robson</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="really liked it"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">really liked it</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span></span>

          <span class="greyText">&nbsp;&middot;&nbsp;</span>
          <a class="lightGreyText" title="Kindle Edition " href="/book/show/39726888-the-thin-man">review of another edition</a>


        

    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer2355572121" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer7055498243119604976">I can’t believe it’s been two years since I read Chandler’s The Big Sleep which I really enjoyed. I was expecting something similar but of course The Thin Man is it’s own creation with marvellous, witty dialogue between husband and wife, Nick and Nora Charles. I love the dryness of Nick:<br/>“Do you suppose he killed her?” Nora asked when I put the paper down again.<br/>“Wynant? I wouldn’t be surprised. He’s batty as hell.”<br/>“Did you know her?”<br/>“Yes. How about a drop of something to cut the phlegm?”<br/>“What was</span>
  <span id="freeText7055498243119604976" style="display:none">I can’t believe it’s been two years since I read Chandler’s The Big Sleep which I really enjoyed. I was expecting something similar but of course The Thin Man is it’s own creation with marvellous, witty dialogue between husband and wife, Nick and Nora Charles. I love the dryness of Nick:<br />“Do you suppose he killed her?” Nora asked when I put the paper down again.<br />“Wynant? I wouldn’t be surprised. He’s batty as hell.”<br />“Did you know her?”<br />“Yes. How about a drop of something to cut the phlegm?”<br />“What was she like?”<br />“Not bad,” I said. “She wasn’t bad-looking and she had a lot of sense and a lot of nerve - and it took both to live with that guy.”<br />“She lived with him?”<br />“Yes. I want a drink, please. That is, it was like that when I knew them.”<br />“Why don’t you have some breakfast first? Was she in love with him or was it just business?”<br />“I don’t know. It’s too early for breakfast.”<br />I love the way Nick keeps asking for a drink and Nora keeps deflecting him. And is there some drinking in this novel!<br />“That afternoon I took Asta for a walk, explained to two people that she was a Schnauzer and not a cross between a Scottie and an Irish Terrier, stopped at Jim’s for a couple of drinks, ran into Larry Crowley, and brought him back to the Normandie with me. Nora was pouring cocktails for the Quinns, Margot Innes, a man whose name I did not catch, and Dorothy Wynant.<br />Dorothy said she wanted to talk to me, so we carried our cocktails into the bedroom.”<br />As you do, lol. The Charles don’t do any sort of work and when it comes to solving the murder of the thin man, Nick keeps his suspicions about the culprit to himself till virtually the end, although he does speculate with Nora:<br />“I think,” she said, “that he’s shielding somebody else.”<br />“Why don’t you think he did it?”<br />She looked surprised. “Because you don’t.”<br />“I said that was a swell reason....”<br />A lot of fun. Highly recommended.</span>
  <a data-text-id="7055498243119604976" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

<div class='carouselRow' style='width: 3600px'>
<ul>
<li class='cover' id='bookCover_2050'>
<a href="https://www.goodreads.com/book/show/2050.Farewell_My_Lovely"><img alt="Farewell, My Lovely" src="https://i.gr-assets.com/images/S/compressed.photo.goodreads.com/books/1465778099l/2050._SX98_.jpg" />
</a>

  
<script type="text/javascript" charset="utf-8">
//<![CDATA[  

  function submitShelfLink(unique_id, book_id, shelf_id, shelf_name, submit_form, exclusive) {
    var checkbox_id = 'shelf_name_' + unique_id + '_' + shelf_id;
    var element = document.getElementById(checkbox_id)

    var checked = element.checked
    if (checked && exclusive) {
      // can't uncheck a radio by clicking it!
      return
    }
    if(document.getElementById("savingMessage")){
      Element.show('savingMessage')
    }
    var element_id = 'shelfInDropdownName_' + unique_id + '_' + shelf_id;
    Element.update(element_id, "saving...");
    if (submit_form) {
      Element.hide('shelfDropdown_' + unique_id)
      var form = document.getElementById('addBookForm' + book_id)
      if (form) {
        form.shelf.value = shelf_name
        form.onsubmit()
      }
    }
    else {
      var action = checked ? 'remove' : ''
      element.checked = !element.checked
      new Ajax.Request('/shelf/add_to_shelf', {asynchronous:true, evalScripts:true, onSuccess:function(request){shelfSubmitted(request, book_id, checkbox_id, element_id, unique_id, shelf_name)}, parameters:'book_id=' + book_id + '&name=' + shelf_name + '&a=' + action + '&authenticity_token=' + encodeURIComponent('uqNi3AHjDQUi/7TJG1S0ShL4dmUz7jzzFb5msNi7TNmLdsnhR4XdH40PeSBYB/1XYw9+MA33sodhGBLgQsfxPQ==')})
    }
  }

  function shelfSubmitted(request, book_id, checkbox_id, element_id, unique_id, shelf_name) {
    Element.update('shelfListfalse_' + book_id, request.responseText)
    afterShelfSave(checkbox_id, element_id, unique_id, shelf_name.escapeHTML())
  }

  function refreshGroupBox(group_id, book_id) {
    new Ajax.Updater('addGroupBooks' + book_id + '', '/group/add_book_box', {asynchronous:true, evalScripts:true, onSuccess:function(request){refreshGroupBoxComplete(request, book_id);}, parameters:'id=' + group_id + '&book_id=' + book_id + '&refresh=true' + '&authenticity_token=' + encodeURIComponent('wCltZg1L2BjMCR/b/cOnfX0obPtDqcbVYpOzEUbN8cHx/MZbSy0IAmP50jK+kO5gDN9krn2wSKEWNcdB3LFMJQ==')})
  }
//]]>
</script>

I\'ve often wondered if all that sugar and caffeine altered my neurochemical makeup. In Farewell, My Lovely, Marlowe deals with the gambling circuit, a murder he stumbles upon, and three very beautiful but potentially deadly women. In The High Window, Marlowe searches the California underworld for a priceless gold coin and finds himself deep in the tangled affairs of a dead coin collector.In all three novels, Chandler’s <div class='bookAuthorProfile__about'>

<span id="freeTextContainer6795169714225646926">Also wrote as Peter Collinson, Daghull Hammett, Samuel Dashiell, Mary Jane Hammett<br/><br/>Samuel Dashiell Hammett was an American author of hardboiled detective novels and short stories. Among the enduring characters he created are Sam Spade (The Maltese Falcon), Nick and Nora Charles (The Thin Man), and the Continental Op (Red Harvest and The Dain Curse). In addition to the significant influence his nove</span>
  <span id="freeText6795169714225646926" style="display:none">Also wrote as Peter Collinson, Daghull Hammett, Samuel Dashiell, Mary Jane Hammett<br /><br />Samuel Dashiell Hammett was an American author of hardboiled detective novels and short stories. Among the enduring characters he created are Sam Spade (The Maltese Falcon), Nick and Nora Charles (The Thin Man), and the Continental Op (Red Harvest and The Dain Curse). In addition to the significant influence his novels and stories had on film, Hammett "is now widely regarded as one of the finest mystery writers of all time" and was called, in his obituary in the New York Times, "the dean of the... 'hard-boiled' school of detective fiction."<br /><br />See <a target="_blank" rel="noopener nofollow" href="http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Dashiell_Hammett">http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Dashiell...</a><br /><br /></span>
  <a data-text-id="6795169714225646926" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>
